Panella, Patrick R. Tamilia, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Mistler, 2005 PA Super 34, 869 A.2d 497, 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 33 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2005)



DISPOSITION: The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment that had affirmed the suppression court's judgment.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Pennsylvania Superior Court entered a judgment that affirmed the suppression court's judgment that held that all evidence obtained as a result of the detentions of defendants in question, a group of students who had attended a fraternity party where alcohol was served, was inadmissible. The Commonwealth then sought to appeal, and the state supreme court granted permission to appeal.


OVERVIEW: Following an undercover operation, police officers issued under-age drinking citations to a group of students, defendants, who were attending a fraternity party where alcohol was served. The officers determined who was underage from the youthful appearance of many of the students. The officers had not obtained a search warrant and did not observe defendants drinking alcohol. Nevertheless, the officers then checked on who was actually under-age for the purposes of alcohol consumption by checking defendant's drivers' licenses for identification. The officers then administered preliminary breath tests and questioned defendants about whether they had been drinking. As a result, under-age drinking citations were issued. The suppression court granted defendants' motion to suppress. The Commonwealth appealed. The trial court then affirmed the suppression court's ruling. On further review, the state supreme court found that since the police officers were engaged in a general search for criminal wrongdoing, their detention of defendants lacked a paramount public interest and the suspicionless stop of them violated their state and federal constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment that had affirmed the suppression court's judgment.

Commonwealth v. Meals


No. 58 MAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


590 Pa. 110; 912 A.2d 213; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2518


December 7, 2005, Argued  
December 27, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on January 26, 2004, at No. 549 MDA 2002, reversing the Judgment of Sentence, in regard to the sexually violent predator determination (otherwise judgment of sentence affirmed), of the Court of Common Pleas of York County entered February 28, 2002, at No. 5375 CA 2000. Appeal allowed June 2, 2005 at 182 MAL 2004. Trial Court Judge: Michael J. Brillhart, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Correale F. Stevens, Maureen Lally-Green, Phylis W. Beck, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Meals, 2004 PA Super 16, 842 A.2d 448, 2004 Pa. Super. LEXIS 31 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered a negotiated plea of guilty to various sexual offenses in exchange for an agreement that he be sentenced to an aggregate term of imprisonment of six to fourteen years. The trial court accepted the pleas and declared defendant to be a sexually violent predator (SVP). The Superior Court (Pennsylvania) reversed the SVP determination. The Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth argued that it proved that defendant was an SVP by clear and convincing evidence and that, in overturning the trial court's judgment, the superior court panel majority impermissibly substituted its judgment for that of the trial court. The supreme court agreed, finding that the superior court erred in reweighing the SVP evidence presented to the trial court, rather than simply assessing the legal sufficiency of the proof respecting SVP status accepted by the trial court, and further erred in requiring greater proof than was required by the statute. Among other things, the supreme court concluded that the superior court stepped beyond its authority when it reweighed the evidence, giving more weight to "absent" factors than to those found and relied upon by the trial court and ignoring the Commonwealth's expert explanation of the relevance of the absent factors. In addition, the supreme court concluded that the superior court 's analysis was problematic because there was no valid basis for its assumption that an expert's diagnosis of pedophilia required more than proof of sexual assaults upon children.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was reversed, and the trial court's SVP determination was reinstated.

Commonwealth v. Bullock


No. 111 MAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


590 Pa. 480; 913 A.2d 207; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2520


October 18, 2006, Argued  
December 27, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bullock v. Pennsylvania, 127 S. Ct. 2262, 167 L. Ed. 2d 1103, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 5288 (U.S., May 14, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on January 14, 2005, reargument denied March 18, 2005, at No. 351 MDA 2004, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal Division, entered on November 17, 2003, at Nos. 893, 894 of 2003. Appeal allowed September 23, 2005 at 280 MAL 2005. Trial Court Judges: Joseph M. Augello, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: John T. Bender, Jack A. Panella, Frank J. Montemuro, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Bullock, 2005 PA Super 16, 868 A.2d 516, 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 16 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty-but-mentally-ill of third degree murder as to a mother and guilty-but-mentally-ill of voluntary manslaughter of an unborn child. Defendant was sentenced to consecutive terms of imprisonment of 15 to 40 years for the murder of the mother and five to 20 years for voluntary manslaughter of the unborn child. The Superior Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed. The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania granted discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the judgment of sentence for voluntary manslaughter of an unborn child, alleging first that the Crimes Against the Unborn Child Act violated due process under the void-for-vagueness doctrine. The supreme court disagreed, finding that the definition of unborn child, which included all stages from gestation to fertilization to live birth, was straightforward and not obscure or difficult to grasp. It was also clear that, by that definition, any viability requirement was eliminated. Defendant also maintained that the statute was unconstitutionally broad because it failed to distinguish between viable living organisms and nonviable or nonliving organisms. After concluding that such a claim sounded in substantive due process, the supreme court held that defendant's liberty interest was not affected by the Act as compliance would not result in confinement. Defendant's challenge under the Equal Protection Clause also failed, as the General Assembly had a legitimate basis for distinguishing between the mother and everyone else. Finally, the supreme court held that trial court's failure to define criminal negligence as it related to the unborn child was harmless error.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence for voluntary manslaughter of the unborn child was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Carson


No. 400 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


590 Pa. 501; 913 A.2d 220; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2555


May 5, 2004, Submitted  
December 27, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Carson, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 282 (Pa., Feb. 9, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Carson v. Pa., 2007 U.S. LEXIS 11182 (U.S., Oct. 9, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order entered on December 26, 2002 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, dismissing PCRA relief at Nos. 1841-1848 May term 1994. Trial Court Judge: William J. Manfredi, Judge.
Commonwealth v. Carson, 559 Pa. 460, 741 A.2d 686, 1999 Pa. LEXIS 3438 (1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, who was sentenced to death, appealed from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Pennsylvania, which dismissed appellant's petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa.C.S. § 9541 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant raised a total of twenty-two claims. Appellant claimed that his direct appeal counsel was ineffective for failing to raise his trial counsel's tolerance of the prosecutor's repeated prejudicial conduct. Regarding the prosecutor's remarks made during his closing arguments, the instant court found that the prosecutor's statement was a fair response to defense counsel's largely improper and baseless implication that the prosecutor would behave unethically, or indeed criminally, in order to win cases. Appellant also claimed that his trial counsel was ineffective for failing to investigate, develop, and present mitigating evidence of his alleged organic brain damage and traumatic childhood. The instant court found that the deficiencies in appellant's proffer were certainly fair game, both as a matter of credibility and as a matter of assessing the ultimate question. But they were matters that should be assessed only after a hearing where the credibility of appellant's experts, his family members, and his trial counsel could properly be evaluated. Accordingly, the appropriate course was to remand this layered ineffectiveness claim to the PCRA court for an evidentiary hearing.


OUTCOME: The court remanded appellant's layered claim of counsel ineffectiveness concerning mitigation evidence to the PCRA court for an evidentiary hearing. In all other respects, the order was affirmed.

Downingtown Area Sch. Dist. v. Chester County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 31 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


590 Pa. 459; 913 A.2d 194; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2514


July 21, 2006, Re-Submitted  
December 27, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered March 20, 2003, at No. 183 C.D. 2002, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County entered December 28, 2001, at No. 00-01233. Appeal allowed March 9, 2004 at 268 MAL 2003. Trial Court Judge: Robert J. Shenkin, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, President Judge, Bernard L. McGinley, Doris A. Smith-Ribner, Dan Pellegrini, Rochelle S. Friedman, Renee Cohn Jubelirer, Robert E. Simpson, JJ.
Downingtown Area Sch. Dist. v. Chester County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 819 A.2d 615, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 165 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2003)



DISPOSITION: The court reversed the order of the lower court, vacated the trial court's decision, and remanded the case to the trial court for consideration of the adequacy of the school district's challenge under the common law construct as elaborated upon in case law outlined by the court and as further reconciled with federal equal protection jurisprudence.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted discretionary review to appellant school district, challenging the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a trial court order upholding an assessment of $ 6,500,000 made by appellee, a county board of assessment appeals, with regard to the real property of appellee owner. The subject property was a neighborhood shopping center located within the school district.


OVERVIEW: In 1996-97, the county assessed the subject property at approximately $ 5,800,000 as part of a county-wide reassessment in which all real estate was assessed at 100 percent of its fair market value. In March 1999, the owner purchased the property for approximately $ 10,400,000. The school district appealed the $ 5,800,000 assessment, and the county board increased the assessment for tax year 2000 to $ 6,500,000. The school district again appealed, seeking to have the assessment further increased to $ 8,500,000. The parties had stipulated that, for tax year 2000, the fair market value of the property was $ 8,500,000 and that the common level ratio for the county was 85.2 percent of fair market value. The trial court upheld the assessment, stating that the school district's proofs regarding comparables was irrelevant. The court held that the trial court erred by refusing to consider the common law procedure for asserting a uniformity challenge as 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 5349(d.2) did not supersede the methods of determining uniformity as established by the court. As such, the trial court erred by disregarding the school district's uncontradicted valuation testimony.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court, vacated the trial court's decision, and remanded the case to the trial court for consideration of the adequacy of the school district's challenge under the common law construct as elaborated upon in case law outlined by the court and as further reconciled with federal equal protection jurisprudence.

 

240 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. White


No. 9 & 10 EAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


589 Pa. 642; 910 A.2d 648; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2274


October 18, 2004, Argued  
November 22, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court dated February 23, 2003 at No. 3282 EDA 2000 quashing in part and reversing in part the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, entered on November 17, 2000 at 9909-0819 1/1. Appeal allowed February 27, 2004 at 152 EAL 2003. Trial Court Judges: Renee Cardwell Hughes, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Kate Ford Elliott, President Judge. Michael T. Joyce and Phyllis W. Beck, JJ. 2003 PA Super 73, 818 A.2d 555 (Pa. Super. Ct. 2003).
Commonwealth v. White, 2003 PA Super 73, 818 A.2d 555, 2003 Pa. Super. LEXIS 299 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, appealed from an order of the Superior Court, Pennsylvania, which concluded that it did not have jurisdiction under Pa.R.A.P. 311(d) to review the recusal motion, and that the Commonwealth had a right to a jury at a degree of guilt hearing.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth asserted the text of Rule 311(d) did not bar review of recusal rulings. The instant court found that the Commonwealth certified in good faith that denial of its recusal motion would substantially handicap its prosecution of this case. Indeed, if the judge was unable to preside and serve as fact-finder impartially, and an unfair verdict was rendered, the Commonwealth, unlike a criminal defendant in a similar circumstance, had no appellate recourse. In considering the merits of the recusal issue, the instant court found the judge should have recused herself. The instant court reasoned that telling the accused that she was going to work hard to do things for her was inappropriate for an impartial jurist. The second question was whether the Commonwealth was entitled to demand a jury at a degree of guilt hearing when a defendant pleaded guilty to murder generally. The instant court found that by pleading guilty to murder generally, appellee waived her right to have a jury as fact-finder in her case. Waiving one's right, however, did not constitute waiver of another's corresponding right; appellee could not vitiate the Commonwealth's right by waving her own.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed in part and affirmed in part. The court reversed the quashal of the Commonwealth's appeal from the denial of its recusal motion and remanded for the appointment of another judge in this matter. The court affirmed the order reversing the denial of the Commonwealth's request for a jury at appellee's degree of guilt hearing.

Commonwealth v. Wilson


No. 91 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


589 Pa. 559; 910 A.2d 10; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2249


May 8, 2006, Submitted  
November 21, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County dated May 28, 2004 and exited June 11, 2004, at No. 2003-1710 granting the motion to quash information. Trial Court Judge: Thomas King Kistler, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth, challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County (Pennsylvania), which granted defendant's motion to quash after the parolee was charged with violating 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9795.2(d)(2) and 9796(e)(2) by failing to register his address with the Pennsylvania State Police and failing to provide the Pennsylvania State Police with the annual address verification.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to aggravated indecent assault, 18 Pa.C.S. § 3125, and indecent assault, 18 Pa.C.S. § 3126, both offenses of which were predicate crimes under Megan's Law II (Act), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9791- 9799.9, 2000 Pa. Laws 74 and subjected defendant to the lifetime reporting requirements. As a result of the violations, the parole was revoked, and defendant was re-sentenced on his original conviction. On appeal, the court found that defendant was subject to the penalty for the underlying offense, the applicable registration provisions of the Act, and the sanctions imposed upon violation of 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9795.2(d)(2) and 9796(e)(2), all of which were plainly visible on the face of the governing statute, and none of which depended on judicial fact-finding under a diminished standard of proof. The reporting requirements and penalties for non-compliance were merely a consequence of defendant's underlying conviction for the predicate offense and raised no Apprendi concerns. Thus, the Act's provisions imposing criminal liability for non-compliance were constitutional. However, defendant's claim of excessive punishment claim was not squarely before the court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court and remanded the case for consideration of defendant's remaining claims delineated in his motion to quash.

 

242 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Fritz v. Wright


No. 116 MAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


589 Pa. 219; 907 A.2d 1083; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2026


April 5, 2006, Argued  
October 18, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on April 11, 2005, at No. 2663 EDA 2003 vacating judgment and remanding case of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Civil Division, dated August 5, 2003 at No. 00-09454. Trial Court Judge: William P. Mahon, J. Intermediate Court Judges: Joseph A. Del Sole, President Judge Emeritus, Susan Peikes Gantman and Peter Paul Olszewski, JJ.
Fritz v. Wright, 2005 PA Super 128, 872 A.2d 851, 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 857 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2005)



DISPOSITION: Order of the Superior Court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured party sued appellees for negligence. Although the jury returned a verdict for appellant, because the same 10 jurors did not agree as to all of the questions material to the verdict and award, appellees moved for a mistrial. The trial court denied the motion; appellees sought review. The Pennsylvania Superior Court vacated the judgment and remanded for a new trial. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Jurors four and eight answered Interrogatory 4 by stating that appellant's contributory negligence was a substantial factor in bringing about his harm. Ten jurors agreed on the amount of damages; jurors four and nine answered Interogatory 6 by stating that he should receive a smaller award. The intermediate appellate court held that the "verdict" on which five-sixths of the jurors had to agree under 42 Pa.C.S. § 5104(b) and Pa. Const. art. I, § 6 was comprised of the total verdict inclusive of each interrogatory response. The high court disagreed. This "same-juror rule" would unconstitutionally burdened a litigant's constitutional right to a jury of 12. The verdict was proper because at least 10 jurors agreed appellees were negligent; at least 10 jurors agreed that appellant's contributory negligence was not a substantial factor in bringing about his harm; and at least 10 jurors agreed as to the amount of damages appellant was entitled to. The same 10 did not have to agree on all these issues. With a general verdict, as long as 10 jurors agreed with the finding and award, there was no issue regarding whether the requisite five-sixths concurred.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed.

In re Twenty-Fourth Statewide Investigating Grand Jury


No. 120 MM 2006, No. 121 MM 2006


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


589 Pa. 89; 907 A.2d 505; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1938; 35 Media L. Rep. 1054


August 29, 2006, Submitted  
October 6, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Trial Court Judge: Barry F. Feudale, Senior Judge.
In re Twenty-Fourth Statewide Investigating Grand Jury Petition of Lancaster Newspapers, Inc., 586 Pa. 465, 895 A.2d 518, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 310 (Pa., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a group of newspapers and a reporter (the newspapers) filed an application for review challenging a grand jury subpoena requiring the production of computer hardware, which application was styled as an emergency. Respondent, the Office of Attorney General, challenged an order requiring disclosure of the notice that it used to submit the underlying investigation to the grand jury.


OVERVIEW: The newspapers were served with two subpoenas issued under the authority of a grand jury, commanding it to produce four computer workstations and two additional computer hard drives. The newspaper initiated proceedings before the judge supervising the grand jury proceedings, seeking to quash the subpoena as overly broad. The supervising judge denied the motion to quash. The newspapers took the position that the order denying its motion to quash was appealable as of right under the collateral order doctrine. The court held that the newspapers attempts to circumvent the subpoena by attempting to invoke the collateral order doctrine, by seeking certification of the supervising judge's order under Pa.R.A.P. 3331(a)(5), and by participating in an arrangement whereby it conditionally surrendered the subject computer hardware were unavailing, because only non-compliance on peril of contempt was the only response the newspapers had available to them. The court noted that other courts have refused similar invitations to employ the collateral order doctrine to circumvent the established course for challenging a subpoena.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order rejecting the newspapers' jurisdictional challenge, without prejudice, and vacated the orders requiring the surrender of the two computer hard drives. The Attorney General's petition for review was denied, and the order staying the Attorney Generals's obligation to comply with the disclosure order was vacated. The court also dismissed the newspapers' request for an exercise of the court's extraordinary jurisdiction.

 

244 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Glen-Gery Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 90 MAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


589 Pa. 135; 907 A.2d 1033; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1894


December 5, 2005, Argued  
September 28, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered July 28, 2004 (Reargument denied September 17, 2004) at No. 155 CD 2004, which affirmed the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Civil Division, entered December 22, 2003, at No. 2003-SU-02356-08. Trial Court Judges: Penny L. Blackwell, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Dan Pellegrini, and Bonnie B. Leadbetter, JJ., and Jess S. Jiuliante, Senior Judge.
Glen-Gery Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd., 856 A.2d 884, 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 567 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner alleged a township's zoning and land use ordinances were invalid due to procedural defects that occurred during their adoption. Appellee zoning hearing board held the challenge was untimely under 42 Pa.C.S. § 5571(c)(5). The trial court affirmed, as did the Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court. The landowner sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Section 909.1(a)(2) (53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10909.1(a)(2)) of the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code and § 5571(c)(5) (42 Pa.C.S. § 5571(c)(5)) of the Pennsylvania Judicial Code both provided for a 30-day time limit on challenges to an ordinance. The owner's challenged was filed between five and seven years after enactment of the ordinance. However, the high court held that a challenge to the procedure in enacting an ordinance could not be dismissed summarily as time-barred because, if the procedural defect implicating constitutional due process concerns such as notice were proven, it would render the ordinance void ab initio. The effect of a finding that the ordinance was void ab initio meant it never became law because of its procedural defects; thus, any component of the challenge that contained a time bar, or intended effective date, was void for having never been properly passed. The board could not establish any reliance on the zoning ordinance; as such, this reason for straying from the void ab initio doctrine was absent. Therefore, the term containing the intended effective date restriction was void if the ordinance was never enacted properly.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the decision of the intermediate appellate court and remanded the matter so that the owner could challenge solely those procedural defects that, if proven, could render the statute void ab initio.

Commonwealth v. Dixon


No. 142 MAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


589 Pa. 28; 907 A.2d 468; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1842


May 10, 2006, Argued  
September 27, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Sipe-Dixon, 932 A.2d 263, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3462 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on March 8, 2005 at No. 1425 MDA 2004 which Affirmed the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County entered on September 7, 2004, at No. CC 121-2004. Trial Court Judge: Richard N. Saxton, Jr., President Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Correale F. Stevens, Maureen Lally-Green, and Zoran Popovich, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Dixon, 876 A.2d 461, 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 973 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a trial court order that denied defendant's petition for release on nominal bail, pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. P. 600(E), after defendant was held in pretrial confinement for more than 180 days. Defendant was charged with rape and numerous sexual offenses against her sons, who were seven and eight at the time of the alleged incidents.


OVERVIEW: The lower court had affirmed the trial court upon concluding that an interlocutory appeal taken by the Commonwealth in the case furnished a basis for extension of the 180-day time limit in accord with a case law decision. The court found that Rule 600(G) and the case law relied upon by the Commonwealth were inapposite. Specifically, the court held that the extensions of time contemplated by the Rule 600(G) and case law were inapplicable when Rule 600(E) was implicated. As such, the court held that Rule 600(E) contemplated an increase in the number of days of pretrial incarceration in excess of 180 based only upon the exclusions of time referenced in Rule 600(C), attributable to the defendant, and not upon extensions of time pursuant to Rule 600(G) for actions taken diligently by the Commonwealth. As a result, defendant was entitled to immediate release on nominal bail after spending 180 days in confinement, exclusive of delays occasioned by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order.

Shepp v. Shepp


No. 242 MAP 2003


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


588 Pa. 691; 906 A.2d 1165; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1939


May 13, 2004, Argued  
September 27, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Shepp v. Shepp, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 4417 (U.S., Apr. 23, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered April 7, 2003 at No. 937 MDA 2002, which affirmed the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Civil Division, entered May 14, 2002, at No. 2002SU0012102C. Trial Court Judge: Stephen P. Linebaugh, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Joan Orie Melvin, Frank J. Montemuro, and Justin M. Johnson, JJ.
Shepp v. Shepp, 2003 PA Super 140, 821 A.2d 635, 2003 Pa. Super. LEXIS 736 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a child custody proceeding, the trial court ordered that appellant, the father, was specifically prohibited while the parties' child was a minor from teaching her about polygamy, plural marriages, or multiple wives. The Pennsylvania Superior Court affirmed the order. The state supreme court granted allocatur to consider issues arising from the order.


OVERVIEW: The father and appellee, the mother, were members of the Mormon Church at the time of the wedding. In the instant divorce proceeding, the mother expressed concern that the father would encourage the parties' daughter to engage in polygamy. This case implicated the free exercise of religion under the First Amendment, and the public policy of ensuring the best interests of children, 32 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5301. The state supreme court held that the Commonwealth's interest in promoting compliance with the statute criminalizing bigamy, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4301, was not an interest of the highest order that would supersede the interest of a parent in speaking to a child about a deeply held aspect of his faith. The state supreme court held that in order to prohibit a parent from advocating religious beliefs which, if acted upon, would constitute a crime, a court had to establish that advocating the prohibited conduct would jeopardize the physical or mental health or safety of the child, or have a potential for significant social burdens. Because such harm was not established in this case, the state supreme court held that there was no constitutional basis for the trial court's order.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed.

Stilp v. Commonwealth


No. 151 MAP 2005, No. 48 EAP 2005, No. 9 MAP 2006


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


588 Pa. 539; 905 A.2d 918; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1736


April 4, 2006, Argued  
September 14, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Stilp v. Commonwealth, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2146 (Pa., Nov. 8, 2006)
Reargument denied by Stilp v. Commonwealth, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2147 (Pa., Nov. 8, 2006)
Related proceeding at Stilp v. Cappy, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 469 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Aug. 15, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Commonwealth Court at No. 404 MD 2005. Order by this Court Assuming Plenary Jurisdiction of the Matter, Dated December 22, 2005. Order by this Court Assuming Plenary Jurisdiction of the Matter, Dated January 11, 2006.
Stilp v. Commonwealth, 585 Pa. 543, 889 A.2d 499, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2973 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a taxpayer and judges, filed three separate actions in the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania against appellees, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, the state treasurer, and legislators, challenging the constitutionality of 2005 Pa. Laws 385, No. 72 (Act 72) and 2005 Pa. Laws 201, No. 44 (Act 44), which had been repealed by Act 72. The present court assumed plenary jurisdiction over the matters.


OVERVIEW: The acts involved compensation for judges, legislators, and officials. The court first held that Act 72 had reduced judicial compensation during judges' terms of office. Moreover, the exception in Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(a) did not apply because Act 72 did not diminish the pay of all salaried public officers of the Commonwealth. The remainder of Act 72's repeal of Act 44 was valid, however, because the act was severable under 1 Pa.C.S. § 1925. Next, the court held that the adoption of Act 44 did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, §§ 1, 2, 3, and 4. The legislative unvouchered expense allowance provision, Act 44, § 2, which amended 46 Pa.C.S. § 1107, did violate Pa. Const. art. II, § 8, which proscribed a mid-term increase in legislative salary. The expense allowance did not bear a reasonable relationship to the actual expenses incurred by individual legislators. The court held that Act 44's nonseverability clause was ineffective, given the separation of powers concerns raised by the clause's inclusion in an act that included compensation provisions for the judiciary. Accordingly, the unconstitutional unvouchered expense provision was severable from the remainder of Act 44.


OUTCOME: The court held that Act 72's repeal of Act 44 was unconstitutional insofar as it reduced judicial compensation; thus, it enjoined Act 72 in part. It declared that the provision of Act 44 respecting mid-term unvouchered expense allowances to legislators was unconstitutional. It ordered that judicial compensation be calculated under Act 44 and that judges be reimbursed for compensation they had lost under Act 72.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Northumberland County (Pennsylvania), which imposed the death penalty after defendant's entry of a plea of guilty to first-degree murder and aggravated assault and a plea of nolo contendere to kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Contrary to defendant's contention, the supreme court held that there was sufficient evidence to support defendant's conviction. In addition, imposition of the death penalty was not unconstitutional, as defendant offered no mitigating evidence to balance against the aggravating circumstance presented by the Commonwealth.

In re Nomination Paper of Nader


No. 198 MAP 2004, No. 17 MAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


588 Pa. 450; 905 A.2d 450; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1546


March 1, 2006, Argued  
August 22, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Nader v. Serody, 549 U.S. 1117, 127 S. Ct. 995, 166 L. Ed. 2d 712, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 123 (2007)
Related proceeding at Nader v. Democratic Nat'l Comm., 555 F. Supp. 2d 137, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 41209 (D.D.C., 2008)
Motion denied by, Motion dismissed by, As moot In re Nomination Paper of Ralph Nader & Peter Miguel Camejo, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1815 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Dec. 4, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered October 14, 2004 at No. 568 MD 2004. Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered January 14, 2005 at No. 568 MD 2004. Trial Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, Commonwealth Court President Judge.
In re Nomination Paper of Nader, 580 Pa. 134, 860 A.2d 1, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2408 (2004)



DISPOSITION: The court quashed the appeal from the first order. It affirmed the second order.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee voters sought to set aside nomination papers filed by appellants, who were candidates for the offices of President and Vice President of the United States. The Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, having set aside the papers, entered two orders requiring appellants, pursuant to 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2937, to pay to the voters transcription and stenography costs in the amount of about $82,000. Appellants challenged the orders imposing costs.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth Court found that wide-ranging fraud and deception had occurred during appellants' signature gathering. It struck nearly two-thirds of the signatures, which included names of cartoon characters and randomly created names, and concluded that the signature-gathering process was the most deceitful and fraudulent exercise ever perpetrated upon it. The court first held that the first order had not disposed of the entire case and thus was not a final order under Pa. R. App. P. 341(d)(1) that was appealable under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 723(a). It therefore quashed the appeal of the first order. As for the second order, appellants were incorrect in arguing that 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2937 did not authorize an award of costs against a candidate whose nomination papers were set aside. The court had not distinguished between "nomination papers" and "nomination petitions" as claimed by appellants. Next, the Commonwealth Court had the authority, under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 764, to adjudicate disputes concerning elections, including the imposition of costs. Finally, the cost provision did not impinge upon any constitutional rights in a way that would warrant constitutional scrutiny.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the appeal from the first order. It affirmed the second order.

Hiller v. Fausey


No. 197 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


588 Pa. 342; 904 A.2d 875; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1543


May 16, 2005, Argued  
August 22, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Fausey v. Hiller, 127 S. Ct. 1876, 167 L. Ed. 2d 363, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 3584 (U.S., Mar. 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on May 25, 2004 at No. 1428 MDA 2003, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County, Civil Division, entered on August 1, 2003 at No. 03-20, 361. Trial Court Judge: Clinton W. Smith, Senior Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Maureen Lally-Green, Susan Peikes Gantman, and James R. Cavanaugh, JJ.2004 PA Super 186, 851 A.2d 193 (Pa. Super. 2004).
Fausey v. Hiller, 2004 PA Super 186, 851 A.2d 193, 2004 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1250 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2004)



DISPOSITION: The court affirmed the order granting the grandmother partial custody of the child.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee maternal grandmother partial custody of the father's child, age eight at the time of the proceeding. The grandmother had filed for partial custody, pursuant to 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5311. A two-day hearing was held and, at its conclusion, the grandmother was granted partial custody of one weekend per month and one week each summer.


OVERVIEW: The child's mother, who was the father's wife and the grandmother's daughter, had died. Prior to the mother's death, the child at issue enjoyed a very close bond with the grandmother, particularly in the two years leading up to the mother's death. The grandmother and the child saw each other daily, with the grandmother providing the child's basic care and helping the child grieve her mother's illness and eventual death. After the mother died, the father refused to let the grandmother see the child. The court found no abuse of discretion with regard to the trial court's grant of partial custody to the grandmother in light of the father not letting the child see the grandmother but for three times in a year after the mother's death. The court also did not think that the father would allow the child and the grandmother to spend time together without a court order. The court held § 5311 constitutional in that the compelling state interest of protecting the health and emotional welfare of a child was protected since § 5311 was narrowly tailored to limit those who seek visitation or partial custody to only a very few situations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting the grandmother partial custody of the child.

Kowenhoven v. County of Allegheny


No. 2 WAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


587 Pa. 545; 901 A.2d 1003; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1281


September 12, 2005, Argued  
July 18, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered April 13, 2004 at No1673CD2003 affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County entered July 10, 2003 at NoGD02-21763. Appeal Allowed February 1, 2005 at 238 WAL 2004. Trial Court Judge: R. Stanton Wettick Jr., Administrative Judge-Civil Division. Intermediate Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, President Judge, Bernard L. McGinley, Dan Pellegrini, Rochelle S. Friedman, Renee Cohn Jubelirer, Robert E. Simpson, M. Hannah Leavitt, JJ.
Kowenhoven v. County of Allegheny, 847 A.2d 172, 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 296 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers filed a class action against appellees, a county and its board of property assessment appeals and review. They argued that the manner in which the county adjudicated appeals violated due process. The trial court dismissed the complaint on the ground that the taxpayers had an adequate remedy at law that precluded injunctive and declaratory relief. The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court should not have dismissed the taxpayers' equitable claims. In order for a court to determine whether equity jurisdiction was proper in the face of an existing legal or statutory remedy, it had to determine if the legal remedy was adequate and complete. Here, the claimed procedural defect raised legal issues independent of the proper valuation of the properties that were the subject of the tax appeals. Moreover, the claimed defect arose from an alleged policy and practice directive setting forth rules that were on their face applicable to all assessment grievances. A multiplicity of individual de novo appeals to the trial court could be avoided through judicial review of the constitutionality of the board's procedures, and the general procedures of which the taxpayers complained could be facially tested against constitutional norms unaided by agency expertise. This same reasoning applied with respect to declaratory relief. A 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim, however, had been properly dismissed. Because equity jurisdiction existed, the taxpayers presently had an adequate state law remedy, removing a prerequisite to their ability to assert a § 1983 suit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Commonwealth Court's order upholding the dismissal of a § 1983 claim. It reversed it in all other respects. The court remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with its opinion.

Town of McCandless v. McCandless Police Officers Ass'n


No. 14 WAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


587 Pa. 525; 901 A.2d 991; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1282; 180 L.R.R.M. 2113


September 12, 2005, Argued  
July 18, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered May 26, 2004 at No. 2623CD2003 affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County entered October 29, 2003 at NoGD03-8284. Appeal Allowed April 19, 2005 at 421 WAL 2004. Trial Court Judge: Joseph M. James. Intermediate Court Judges: Bernard L. McGinley, Dan Pellegrini, JJ and James R. Kelley, Senior Judge.
Town of McCandless v. McCandless Police Officers Ass'n, 851 A.2d 265, 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 453 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee collective bargaining representative filed a grievance under Act 111, 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 217.1-217.10, against appellant town on behalf of a terminated police officer. An arbitrator held that the grievance was not moot. On appeal, the trial court dismissed the town's petition for review. The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed the dismissal. The town appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court granted limited review to consider whether a pending grievance filed in opposition to an Act 111 employee's termination was rendered moot when the employee failed to grieve a separate, subsequent set of charges that also sought his termination. The court stated that the proper resolution of the appeal required consideration of a predicate question: the level of deference due to an arbitrator's decision on mootness. The scope of review applicable to Act 111 grievance arbitration appeals was narrow certiorari review, which allowed the court to inquire into only four areas: the jurisdiction of the arbitrator, the regularity of the proceedings, whether the arbitrator had exceeded his powers, and whether there had been a deprivation of constitutional rights. Mootness was not an issue that implicated arbitral jurisdiction; rather, it posed a question of justiciability. There was no dispute here that the initial grievance fell within the arbitrator's jurisdiction. Accordingly, the question of mootness was for the arbitrator, and it did not fall within the court's scope of review. Therefore, the arbitrator's decision had to stand.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the Commonwealth Court.

Pa. Tpk. Comm'n v. Commonwealth


No. 143 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


587 Pa. 347; 899 A.2d 1085; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 996; 179 L.R.R.M. 3162


March 2, 2006, Argued  
June 19, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court on July 30, 2004 as amended August 4, 2004 at No. 641 M.D. 2003. Intermediate Court Judges: Bernard L. McGinley, Dan Pellegrini, JJ. and James R. Kelley, Senior Judge. 855 A.2d 923 (Pa. Cmwlth. 2004).
Pa. Tpk. Comm'n v. Commonwealth, 855 A.2d 923, 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 574 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Pennsylvania Turnpike Commission sued defendant labor union and others, seeking a judgment declaring the Pennsylvania First-Level Supervisor Collective Bargaining Act, 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 1103.101-1103.701, unconstitutional. The Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court held that the Act violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 32. The union appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Act mandated collective bargaining with the Commission's first-level supervisors. The lower court held that as there was no rational reason to treat first-level supervisors of the Commission differently from first-level supervisors employed by any other public employer, the Act was special legislation that violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 32. The high court agreed. There was nothing distinctive about the Commission and its relationship with its first-level supervisors that separated it from all other public employers, thus requiring different treatment when it came to collective bargaining. The Act violated art. III, § 32, because its narrow classification did not bear a reasonable relationship to its purpose. And as Act created a class with one member and did so in a fashion that made it impossible for another member to join the class, it was also unconstitutional per se.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. May


No. 415 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


587 Pa. 184; 898 A.2d 559; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 850


May 25, 2004, Submitted  
May 25, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by May v. Pennsylvania, 549 U.S. 1022, 127 S. Ct. 557, 166 L. Ed. 2d 414, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8425 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order entered on March 26, 2003 in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Lebanon County, dismissing the PCRA petition at No. 1990-10071. Trial Court Judge: Samuel A. Kline, Judge.
Commonwealth v. May, 551 Pa. 286, 710 A.2d 44, 1998 Pa. LEXIS 584 (1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lebanon County (PCRA court), which denied the inmate's petition for post-conviction relief pursuant to the Post-Conviction Relief Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9541 et seq. The inmate was convicted of first-degree murder. ON direct appeal, the court affirmed the conviction, reversed the sentence, and the inmate was eventually resentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: The inmate's conviction arose from rape and stabbing death of a young woman, whose body was not found for several years. The inmate raised numerous contentions in his petition, some stemming from his 1991 trial and direct appeal therefrom, and others arising from the 1995 re-sentencing. The court agreed that the trial court erred during the 1995 re-sentencing hearing by precluding the inmate's proffered mitigation evidence of childhood abuse. The court found that the inmate's trial counsel was ineffective for failing to object to the trial court's ruling and appellate counsel were ineffective for failing to appeal the decision of the trial court on re-sentencing. The court held that the childhood abuse evidence was admissible pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(e)(8). The court noted that the trial court doubted whether the proposed evidence fit into one of the mitigating factors set forth in § 9711 but erred in sustaining the Commonwealth's objection to the same. The court held that not only was such evidence relevant, its presence was a necessary component of a fair and complete sentencing proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, in part, the judgment of sentence, and remanded the case to the PCRA court for a new penalty phase. Otherwise, the court affirmed the inmate's conviction.

Commonwealth v. Spotz


No. 338 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


587 Pa. 1; 896 A.2d 1191; 2006 Pa. LEXIS 659


May 20, 2002, Submitted  
May 2, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Schuylkill County, Criminal Division, entered April 5, 2001 at No. 269-1995.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Schuylkill County (Pennsylvania), which denied defendant's petition for post conviction relief, pursuant to the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9546. Defendant was found guilty of first-degree murder, aggravated assault, kidnapping, robbery of a motor vehicle, robbery, theft by unlawful taking, and criminal conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions arose from a killing spree spanning three days wherein he murdered four people, including his brother. The underlying charges in the case at bar involved the murder of a convenience store clerk. After murdering his brother, defendant and his girlfriend fled, with defendant shooting the convenience store clerk. Defendant took the victim's car but was eventually apprehended. Defendant raised numerous arguments of error on appeal, including 12 substantive issues, plus a claim that he did not receive independent appellate review, a generalized claim of ineffectiveness of counsel, and a claim of cumulative error. The court found no merit in any of defendant's contentions of error. Particularly, the court found no error with regard to the trial court's refusal to join the charges against defendant arising from the four murders since, though evidence of the murder of the brother was relevant and admissible in the trial at bar to show motive, intent, identity, and sequence of events as to the murder of the convenience store clerk, the crimes were distinct and not a part of the same sequence of events, therefore compulsory joinder was not required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the PCRA court.

 

256 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Peters v. Costello


No. 8 EAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


586 Pa. 102; 891 A.2d 705; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 3199


July 27, 2004, Submitted  
December 30, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered August 11, 2003, at 3850 EDA 2002, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County entered November 15, 2002, at D.R. No. 0C9900866.
Peters v. Costello, 833 A.2d 1156, 2003 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3986 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of the order of the Pennsylvania Superior Court affirming the order finding that appellee applicants had standing to seek partial custody or visitation under the Pennsylvania Grandparent Visitation Act, 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5311 et seq.


OVERVIEW: During her childhood, the mother resided almost continuously with the applicants, who were not her biological parents. While residing with the applicants, the mother, who was not married to the father, had a baby. During the first four years of the child's life, the mother and child resided with the applicants. After the father obtained primary physical custody of the child, the father denied the applicants access to the child. The trial court did not err in finding that the applicants had standing to seek partial custody or visitation under 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5313(a), which gave grandparents the right to seek partial custody, visitation rights, or both where the child had resided with them for a period of 12 months or more and was subsequently removed by a parent. The applicants stood in loco parentis to the mother, as they raised her. Section 5313(a) did not define grandparent. The common usage of parent, consulted under 1 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1903(a), included reference to a person standing in loco parentis to the child. The applicants, who stood in loco parentis to the mother of the child, retained the same rights as other grandparents.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Wesley United Methodist Church v. Dauphin County Bd. of Assessment Appeals

SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

Trial Court Judge: Bruce F. Bratton, J. Intermediate Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, President Judge. Bernard L. McGinley, Doris A. Smith-Ribner, Dan Pellegrini, Rochelle S. Friedman, Bonnie B. Leadbetter, Renee Cohn Jubelirer and Robert E. Simpson, JJ. 844 A.2d 57 (Pa. Cmwlth. Ct. 2004).
Wesley United Methodist Church v. Dauphin County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 844 A.2d 57, 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 154 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county board of assessment appeals sought review of an order entered by the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of the Dauphin County Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division (Pennsylvania), which held that two parcels containing a parking lot used by appellee church were exempt from real estate taxation.


OVERVIEW: After the board had denied the church's request for a tax exemption for two immediately adjacent parcels of land where its parking lot was located, the church appealed to the common pleas court. At a de novo hearing, the testimony indicated that limited off-street parking existed near the church. The common pleas court held that the parking lot was reasonably necessary for the occupancy of the church and thus was tax-exempt. The commonwealth court affirmed. On further appeal, the court affirmed. Under § 204(a)(1) of the General County Assessment Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5020-204(a)(1), which had been enacted pursuant to Pa. Const. art. VII, § 2(a), the court held that the parking lot was properly held to be tax-exempt as such property was necessary for the occupancy and enjoyment of the church based on the number of members who did not live within walking distance of the church. The court noted that, without the needed parking spaces, the church's ability to exist would be impeded. The court concluded that not all church parking lots would be entitled to tax-exempt status; proof of the parking lots' reasonable necessity to the existence of a church itself was needed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the commonwealth court's order.

Commonwealth v. Duffey


No. 324 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


585 Pa. 493; 889 A.2d 56; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 3082


May 28, 2002, Submitted  
December 28, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at Duffey v. Beard, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 102322 (M.D. Pa., Sept. 12, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County, Criminal Division at No. 84 CR 176 entered on 12/19/2000.
Commonwealth v. Duffey, 579 Pa. 186, 855 A.2d 764, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 1898 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After remanding the matter for an evidentiary hearing, before the court was the petition for post-conviction relief filed by appellant, pursuant to the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-46. Appellant's petition was originally denied but the remand was ordered after appellant's penalty phase issues were deemed to require further hearing. Appellant was sentenced to death for murder.


OVERVIEW: Appellant presented a layered claim of ineffectiveness related to the testimony of the Commonwealth's rebuttal witness, a doctor. The court had found that the expert's testimony unconstitutionally commented on appellant's right to remain silent and that defendant was prejudiced by the impermissible reference. However, the PCRA court found that trial counsel's strategy in failing to object to the unconstitutional comments was reasonable as it was found to have been supported by trial counsel's strategy in not wanting to undermine his credibility with the jury by objecting to the expert and by using the testimony as evidence of appellant's limited mental capacity. The PCRA court did find that appellate counsel was ineffective but, because appellant failed to prove his underlying claim of trial counsel ineffectiveness, he was unable to prove his appellate counsel ineffectiveness claim. Appellant's penalty phase issues as to trial counsel failing to present mitigating circumstances to the jury were found meritless and his sentence was deemed proportional.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that appellant was not entitled to relief on his petition and denied the same.

Pittsburgh Palisades Park, LLC v. Commonwealth


No. 27 MM 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


585 Pa. 196; 888 A.2d 655; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 3099


May 18, 2005, Submitted  
December 28, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Seeking Declaratory and Injunctive Relief.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and some elected officials, filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer to the petition in which petitioners, a corporation and an interest owner, alleged that 4 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1209 violated the Pennsylvania Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The corporation and interest owner purchased property and allegedly intended to apply for a gaming license. The corporation and owner alleged that 4 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1209 of the Pennsylvania Race Horse Development and Gaming Act, 4 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1101 et seq., was unconstitutional. Section 1209 allegedly forbade the legislature from altering, amending, or repealing certain provisions of the Act. This allegedly contravened Pa. Const. art. II, § 1, which prohibited dilution of the rights of the sovereign. The corporation and owner lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of 4 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1209. The interest of the corporation and owner was not substantial, as it did not appear to be greater than any other citizen's interest. Section 1209 did not harm them. As the corporation and owner did not establish that § 1209 harmed them or would harm them, they failed to demonstrate that they had an immediate interest. The corporation and owner did not establish taxpayer standing. The constitutionality of § 1209 was not to go unchallenged if standing was not granted, as the constitutionality of the Act had been hotly contested.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections were sustained.

Hosp. & Healthsystem Ass'n of Pa. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare


No. 219 MAP 2003


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


585 Pa. 106; 888 A.2d 601; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2975


November 30, 2004, Argued  
December 27, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered July 23, 2003 at No. 840 MD 2002. Trial Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, President Judge. Doris A. Smith-Ribner, Dan Pellegrini, Rochelle S. Friedman, Hannah M. Leavitt, Renee Cohn & Robert E. Simpson, JJ. 828 A.2d 1196 (Pa. Cmwlth. 2003).
Hosp. & Healthsystem Assn of Pa. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare, 828 A.2d 1196, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 524 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant health care providers filed a complaint seeking to enjoin provisions of a general appropriations bill as violative of Pa. Const. art. III, § 11. The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) granted appellee government representatives' preliminary objections, concluding that the bill did not contain substantive language that violated art. III, § 11. The health care providers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The health care providers filed a complaint to enjoin certain provisions of the General Appropriations Act of 2002 (2002 GAA) on the ground that it violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 11. Specifically, the health care providers asserted that 2002 GAA violated that constitutional provision because the 2002 GAA contained improper substantive language. The health care providers contended that 2002 GAA attempted to impose a default rate as the rate that the non-plan providers such as the health care providers had to accept for emergency services even though the Quality Healthcare Accountability and Protection Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 40, §§ 991.2101-991.2193 (Act), required that providers be paid "all reasonably necessary costs" for the time period covered by 2002 GAA. The trial court found that the 2002 GAA did not contain improper substantive language. On appeal, the state supreme court cited Biles and found that to be constitutional, the language in an appropriations bill must not conflict with existing law. The state supreme court then found that the 2002 GAA did conflict with existing law, the Act, due to the differences in the language regarding the reimbursement rate requirement.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Killinger


No. 10 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


585 Pa. 92; 888 A.2d 592; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2988


November 9, 2004, Submitted  
December 27, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Centre County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, at No. 2003-1339 Entered December 31, 2003. Trial Court Judge: Thomas King Kistler, Judge. 64 Pa. D. & C.4th 369 (Centre Co. 2003).
Commonwealth v. Killinger, 64 Pa. D. & C.4th 369, 2003 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 167 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the order of the Centre County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which held that lifetime probation imposed against non-sexually violent predators (SVP) offenders under Pennsylvania's Registration of Sexual Offenders Act (Megan's Law II), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9795.2 (2000) was unconstitutional. Appellee was convicted of indecent assault, a predicate crime under Megan's Law II.


OVERVIEW: Because appellee was convicted of a predicate crime under Megan's Law II, he was required to register with the Pennsylvania State Police upon his release on parole and to verify any change of his registered address within 10 days. The Commonwealth charged appellee with two violations of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9795.2(a)(2)(i) for failing to inform the state police of his change in address. Appellee filed a pre-trial motion seeking dismissal of the charges on the basis that the penalty provision stated in § 9795.2(d)(1) was unconstitutional. The trial court agreed with appellee, holding that the penalty provision of § 9795.2(d)(1), which treated a violation as a third-degree felony with a statutory maximum penalty of seven years' incarceration, as increased incarceration. The court disagreed with the trial court's analysis and concluded that the trial court erred by applying the caselaw on the punitive nature of mandatory probation upon SVP offenders to the case at bar. In the instant case, appellee committed a predicate offense and was subjected to such punishment as attached by law to that offense upon conviction, a punishment that was clearly set forth in the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Eagle Envtl. II, L.P. v. Commonwealth

584 Pa. 494; 884 A.2d 867; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2392


May 11, 2004, Argued

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 261 MAP 2003

Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered February 10, 2003 at No. 1180CD2002 which Affirmed the Order of the Environmental Hearing Board entered April 4, 2002 at No. 2001-198-MG. 818 A.2d 574 (2003). Appeal allowed November 12, 2003 at 173 MAL 2003.

Intermediate Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, President Judge, Bernard L. McGinley, Doris A. Smith-Ribner, Rochelle S. Friedman, Bonnie B. Leadbetter, Renee L. Cohn and Hannah M. Leavitt, JJ.

263 MAP 2003

Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered February 10, 2003 at No. 1179CD2002 which Affirmed the Order of the Environmental Hearing Board entered April 11, 2002 at No. 2001-252-R. 818 A.2d 574 (2003).
Tri-County Indus. v. Dep't of Envtl. Prot., 818 A.2d 574, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 85 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landfill operators challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that the regulations of appellee, the Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Protection (DEP), adopting a harms/benefits test as part of the permitting process for waste disposal facilities was constitutional and authorized by statute. Appellee, a township Environmental Hearing Board (EHB), had upheld the DEP's grant of a conditional permit.


OVERVIEW: With regard to the application of one of the operators, the DEP found that the benefits outweighed the harms, in part, because the harms were only potentialities, whereas the benefits were actual, and granted the conditional permit. The permit included a condition that stated the failure to provide for all benefits described in the submissions would invalidate the harms/benefits analysis and violate the permit. The operator appealed to the EHB, which denied the operator's motion for summary judgment, upholding the validity of the harms/benefits test. On appeal, the operators asserted that the harms/benefits test was outside the authority granted to the DEP by the legislature. The court disagreed and held that the balancing test satisfied the DEP's requirement to establish a flexible and effective means to implement and enforce the provisions of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 35, § 6018.102(5) and Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 4000.102(b)(4). The court noted that the test applied to an extremely narrow subject matter and was well within the police power of the State.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

Kramer v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Rite Aid Corp.)


No. 51 MAP 2003, No. 52 MAP 2003


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


584 Pa. 309; 883 A.2d 518; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2156


December 2, 2003, Argued  
September 28, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on 2/22/02 at No. 1718 CD 2001 which reversed the Decision of the Workers' Compensation Appeal Board dated 6/26/01 at No. A00-0350. Intermediate Court Judges: Dan Pellegrini and Rochelle S. Friedman, JJ., and Jim Flaherty, Senior Judge.
Kramer v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Rite Aid Corp.), 794 A.2d 953, 2002 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 93 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court reversed a decision which had upheld the workers' compensation judge's ruling that found appellant employer, a privately-insured employer, properly offset the workers' compensation benefits it gave to appellee claimant by the severance payment the employer made to her. The employer appealed the ruling, while the claimant charged that the statute allowing the offset violated her equal protection rights.


OVERVIEW: The claimant sustained a work-related neck injury. She started to receive workers' compensation benefits. The employer then relocated its facility; as a consequence, the claimant was laid off. Following the layoff, the employer reinstated her workers' compensation benefits, which had been terminated when she had returned to work. Thereafter, she received a check from the employer for the net amount of severance pay due to her as a result of the employer's relocation. The payment was part of a severance agreement contained in an addendum to the most recent collective bargaining agreement that applied to her. The employer soon notified her that it would be offsetting the workers' compensation benefits by the severance pay she had received. The workers' compensation judge found that the employer, a privately-insured employer, could do so. An administrative appeals board affirmed. The Commonwealth Court disagreed and reversed. On further review, the state supreme court found both privately-insured and self-insured employers were entitled to the offset pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 71(a) and that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 71(a) did not violate her equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The Commonwealth Court's ruling was reversed and the decision of the workers' compensation judge finding the employer properly took an offset against the claimant's workers' compensation benefits in the amount of the severance payment made by the employer to the claimant was reinstated.

Commonwealth v. O'Berg


No. 92 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


584 Pa. 11; 880 A.2d 597; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1727


November 9, 2004, Submitted  
August 16, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered September 10, 2003, at No. 797 MDA 2001, affirmed the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Criminal Division, entered December 14, 2000, at No. 2000-1103. Appeal allowed June 29, 2004 at 945 MAL 2003. Trial Court Judge: Charles C. Brown, Jr., President Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Joseph A. Hudock, Debra Todd and Zoran Popovich, JJ.
Commonwealth v. O'Berg, 839 A.2d 1160, 2003 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4927 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court (Pennsylvania) entered a judgment that dismissed defendant's claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence in a case where defendant had been convicted by a jury of misdemeanor disorderly conduct, misdemeanor failure of a disorderly person to disperse, and the summary offense of criminal mischief. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant participated in a street riot. As a result, he was charged with the misdemeanor offenses of disorderly conduct and failure of a disorderly person to disperse. He was also charged with the summary offense of criminal mischief. Following a trial, a jury convicted defendant of all three charges. The trial court then sentenced defendant to 30 days to 23-and-a-half months in prison. Although he could have chosen to remain free on bail, he opted to begin serving his sentence. Defendant then filed a post-sentence motion, which was denied. New counsel appeared on his behalf, and filed a brief challenging the sufficiency of the evidence and raising an issue of the effectiveness of defendant's trial counsel. The Superior Court found the evidence supported the convictions, affirmed the judgment of sentence, and found the ineffectiveness claim was barred by the general rule announced in Grant that an ineffectiveness claim should be deferred until a collateral review proceeding. On further review, the state supreme court declined to recognize a "short sentence" exception to the Grant rule because it concluded that such an exception would not be workable in most cases.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court found that the evidence supported the convictions, affirmed the judgment of sentence, and declined to recognize a "short sentence" exception to the general rule that claims of ineffective assistance of counsel should be deferred until collateral review proceedings.

Alexander v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 51 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


583 Pa. 592; 880 A.2d 552; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1722; 15 A.L.R.6th 843


October 20, 2004, Argued  
August 15, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered March 19, 2003, at 1546 C.D. 2002, which Affirmed the Order of Chester County Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division entered May 22, 2002 at Docket No. 02-00536. 822 A.2d 92 (Pa. Cmwlth. 2003). Appeal Allowed April 7, 2004 at 371 MAL 2003. Trial Court Judges: Lawrence E. Wood, Senior Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, President Judge. Rochelle S. Friedman, J. Jim Flaherty, Senior Judge.
Alexander v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing, 822 A.2d 92, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 158 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing (the Department) appealed the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a trial court order that held the Department unlawfully applied the Interlock Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7001 et seq. (2001), to appellee driver.


OVERVIEW: The driver had three DUI offenses, one of which occurred after the Interlock Law was enacted. The lower court held that it was an unlawfully retroactive application of the Interlock Law to enforce the interlock requirement on the basis of convictions which occurred before the statute was enacted. The court disagreed and held that the Department did not err in considering the driver's two prior DUI convictions as predicate offenses for purposes of determining his status as a recidivist offender under the Interlock Law. As such, the Department's notice to the driver that he was required to comply with the Interlock Law, or have his license suspended for an additional year, was lawful. As a result, the trial court erred in striking the interlock requirement and the lower court erred in affirming that order. The court noted that no statute was considered retroactive unless the Pennsylvania General Assembly so stated and that no substantial right of the driver was impaired in that driving was a privilege and the law granted the equivalent to a probationary license.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order.

 

267 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Beaman


No. 34 WAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


583 Pa. 636; 880 A.2d 578; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1726


March 7, 2005, Argued  
August 15, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered March 31, 2004 at No. 415 WDA 2003 affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County entered February 10, 2003 at No. CC 200110942. Appeal allowed October 5, 2004 at 221 WAL 2004. Trial Court Judge: Donna Jo McDaniel, Administrative Judge, Criminal Division. Intermediate Court Judges: Joan Orie Melvin, Peter Paul Olszewski and Zoran Popovich, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Beaman, 2004 PA Super 91, 846 A.2d 764, 2004 Pa. Super. LEXIS 314 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Pennsylvania Superior Court affirming the judgment of sentence imposed upon him after defendant was convicted of driving under the influence in violation of former 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(1), (4).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped at a driving under the influence checkpoint. Defendant filed an unsuccessful motion to suppress, claiming that sobriety checkpoints were per se violative of Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. Defendant provided statistical data comparing sobriety checkpoint efficiency with roving patrols, arguing that the statistics demonstrated that there existed a practical alternative to roadblocks. Because of the existence of a practical alternative, defendant argued that the three-factor balancing test previously applied in roadblock cases was inapplicable. Under this test, a court weighed the gravity of the public concerns served by the seizure, the degree to which the seizure advanced the public interest, and the severity of the interference with individual liberty. The reviewing court reaffirmed the use of the three-pronged balancing test in such cases. Driving under the influence roadblocks were not so ineffective as to be constitutionally unreasonable under Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. While defendant's statistics may have shown that roving patrols required less man hours than roadblocks, the statistics did not take into account the deterrent effect of roadblocks.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

268 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Shiffler


No. 32 MAP 2004


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


583 Pa. 478; 879 A.2d 185; 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1496


November 30, 2004, Argued  
July 22, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the order of the Superior Court, No. 1648 MDA 2002, dated October 3, 2003, vacating and remanding the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, No. 0676 of 2002, imposed October 1, 2002.Appeal allowed March 10, 2004 at 818 MAL 2003. Trial Court Judge: Paul K. Allison, J. Intermediate Judeges: Michael T. Joyce, Richard B. Klien and Stephen J. McEwen, Jr., JJ. 2003 PA Super 371, 833 A.2d 1128 (Pa. Super. 2003).
Commonwealth v. Shiffler, 2003 PA Super 371, 833 A.2d 1128, 2003 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3259 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to burglary, aggravated assault, theft, and resisting arrest, and was sentenced. The Commonwealth appealed the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which vacated and remanded the trial court's judgment of sentence. The state supreme court granted allocatur to consider whether the recidivist philosophy influenced or controlled the interpretation of the three strikes law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9714.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the issue was whether the three strikes law reflected a "recidivist philosophy" and should be construed to allow for heightened punishment for repeat offenders only where their convictions for crimes of violence, and corresponding terms of incarceration, were sequential and each was separated by an intervening opportunity to reform. Specifically, defendant argued that it would be absurd to treat him as a third-time offender where he had never been sentenced as a second-time offender. The state supreme court found that at the time of defendant's concurrent sentencing for his three prior burglaries in 1997, he was not sentenced as a second-time offender. The anomaly of defendant's situation was that while he had never been sentenced as a second-strike offender, application of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9714(a)(2) would subject him to sentencing as a third-strike offender. The legislative purpose of the three strikes law was to punish multiple offenders consistently with the recidivist philosophy. Thus, because 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9714(a)(2) was ambiguously silent regarding whether predicate convictions had to be sequential, that ambiguity was construed in defendant's favor.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court vacated the order of the superior court and remanded the matter to the trial court for re-sentencing.
